            function createLink(src) {
                if ("connection" in navigator) {
                    if (navigator.connection.saveData != true) {
                        var preloadLink = document.createElement("link");
                        preloadLink.rel = "preload";
                        preloadLink.href = src;

                        preloadLink.type = "font/woff2";
                        preloadLink.crossOrigin = "";
                        document.head.insertBefore(preloadLink, document.head.firstChild);
                    }
                }
            }

        </script>

<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Long-read sequencing reveals genomic structural variations that underlie creation of quality protein maize"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2020 11:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-01-07"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Mutation of o2 doubles maize endosperm lysine content, but it causes an inferior kernel phenotype. Developing quality protein maize (QPM) by introgressing o2 modifiers (Mo2s) into the o2 mutant benefits millions of people in developing countries where maize is a primary protein source. Here, we report genome sequence and annotation of a South African QPM line K0326Y, which is assembled from single-molecule, real-time shotgun sequencing reads collinear with an optical map. We achieve a N50 contig length of 7.7 million bases (Mb) directly from long-read assembly, compared to those of 1.04&#8201;Mb for B73 and 1.48&#8201;Mb for Mo17. To characterize Mo2s, we map QTLs to chromosomes 1, 6, 7, and 9 using an F2 population derived from crossing K0326Y and W64Ao2. RNA-seq analysis of QPM and o2 endosperms reveals a group of differentially expressed genes that coincide with Mo2 QTLs, suggesting a potential role in vitreous endosperm formation. The South African quality protein maize (QPM) cultivars have the desired high lysine content and kernel hardness due to o2 mutation and the introgression of modifiers of o2 (Mo2) QTLs, respectively. Here, the authors assemble the genome of a QPM line and identify candidate genes underlying Mo2 QTLs."/>

            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0002-1733-4619"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0002-1733-4619</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff6">6</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="University of Chinese Academy of Sciences" /><meta itemprop="address" content="BGI Education Center, University of Chinese Academy of Sciences, Shenzhen, 518083, China" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Hongjun-Liu" data-author-popup="auth-Hongjun-Liu">Hongjun Liu</a></span><span class="u-js-hide"> 
            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0001-5123-688X"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0001-5123-688X</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff7">7</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Shandong Agricultural University" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0000 9482 4676, grid.440622.6, State Key Laboratory of Crop Biology, College of Life Sciences, Shandong Agricultural University, Tai’an, 271018, China" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Yubin-Li" data-author-popup="auth-Yubin-Li">Yubin Li</a></span><span class="u-js-hide"> 
            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0002-9795-9933"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0002-9795-9933</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff8">8</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Qingdao Agricultural University" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0000 9526 6338, grid.412608.9, College of Agronomy, Qingdao Agricultural University, Qingdao, 266109, China" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Qiong-Wang" data-author-popup="auth-Qiong-Wang">Qiong Wang</a></span><span class="u-js-hide"> 
            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0003-2277-7104"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0003-2277-7104</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff2">2</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Shanghai Institutes for Biological Sciences, Chinese Academy of Sciences" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0004 0467 2285, grid.419092.7, National Key Laboratory of Plant Molecular Genetics, CAS Center for Excellence in Molecular Plant Sciences, Institute of Plant Physiology &amp; Ecology, Shanghai Institutes for Biological Sciences, Chinese Academy of Sciences, Shanghai, 200032, China" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Chunguang-Du" data-author-popup="auth-Chunguang-Du">Chunguang Du</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff9">9</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Montclair State University" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0001 0745 9736, grid.260201.7, Department of Biology, Montclair State University, Montclair, NJ, 07043, USA" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Joachim-Messing" data-author-popup="auth-Joachim-Messing">Joachim Messing</a></span><span class="u-js-hide"> 
            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0001-8260-2643"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0001-8260-2643</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff5">5</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Rutgers University" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0004 1936 8796, grid.430387.b, Waksman Institute of Microbiology, Rutgers University, 190 Frelinghuysen Road, Piscataway, NJ, 08854, USA" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Brian_A_-Larkins" data-author-popup="auth-Brian_A_-Larkins">Brian A. Larkins</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff10">10</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="University of Arizona" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0001 2168 186X, grid.134563.6, School of Plant Sciences, University of Arizona, Tucson, AZ, 85721, USA" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Yongrui-Wu" data-author-popup="auth-Yongrui-Wu" data-corresp-id="c1">Yongrui Wu<svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-email"></use></svg></a></span><span class="u-js-hide"> 
            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0003-3822-0511"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0003-3822-0511</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff2">2</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Shanghai Institutes for Biological Sciences, Chinese Academy of Sciences" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0004 0467 2285, grid.419092.7, National Key Laboratory of Plant Molecular Genetics, CAS Center for Excellence in Molecular Plant Sciences, Institute of Plant Physiology &amp; Ecology, Shanghai Institutes for Biological Sciences, Chinese Academy of Sciences, Shanghai, 200032, China" /></span></sup> &amp; </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Wenqin-Wang" data-author-popup="auth-Wenqin-Wang" data-corresp-id="c2">Wenqin Wang<svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-email"></use></svg></a></span><span class="u-js-hide"> 
            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0001-6427-6338"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0001-6427-6338</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Shanghai Jiao Tong University" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0004 0368 8293, grid.16821.3c, School of Agriculture and Biology, Shanghai Jiao Tong University, Shanghai, 200240, China" /></span></sup> </li></ul>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Mutation of <i>o2</i> doubles maize endosperm lysine content, but it causes an inferior kernel phenotype. Developing quality protein maize (QPM) by introgressing <i>o2 modifier</i>s (<i>Mo2</i>s) into the <i>o2</i> mutant benefits millions of people in developing countries where maize is a primary protein source. Here, we report genome sequence and annotation of a South African QPM line K0326Y, which is assembled from single-molecule, real-time shotgun sequencing reads collinear with an optical map. We achieve a N50 contig length of 7.7 million bases (Mb) directly from long-read assembly, compared to those of 1.04 Mb for B73 and 1.48 Mb for Mo17. To characterize <i>Mo2</i>s, we map QTLs to chromosomes 1, 6, 7, and 9 using an F<sub>2</sub> population derived from crossing K0326Y and W64A<i>o2</i>. RNA-seq analysis of QPM and <i>o2</i> endosperms reveals a group of differentially expressed genes that coincide with <i>Mo2</i> QTLs, suggesting a potential role in vitreous endosperm formation.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Shotgun DNA sequencing has been used to build contiguous sequence information of entire chromosomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Messing, J., Crea, R. &amp; Seeburg, P. H. A system for shotgun DNA sequencing. Nucleic Acids Res. 9, 309–321 (1981)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d13290e695">1</a></sup>. However, previous sequencing reads were too short to resolve the order of repetitive sequences, because the repeats were longer than an individual read, or sequence variations between two tandem copies were not of sufficient length to distinguish them. A recent platform with long reads from single DNA molecules can overcome the low resolution of reconstructed repetitive regions in complex plant genomes, which allows researchers to produce a genome with the fewest gaps of any previously sequenced maize genome and a high-resolution sequence analysis demonstrating allelic diversity.</p><p>It is well known that the most abundant storage proteins in maize endosperm, prolamins called zeins, are deficient in the essential amino acids, lysine and tryptophan, requiring costly dietary supplementation. A natural maize high-lysine variant lacks a functional Opaque2 (O2) bZIP transcription factor that regulates expression of most zein genes. In the <i>o2</i> mutant, the amount of zein proteins is dramatically reduced, but is compensated by an increase of other proteins (non-zeins), thereby doubling the lysine levels of total protein compared to normal maize<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Gibbon, B. C. &amp; Larkins, B. A. Molecular genetic approaches to developing quality protein maize. Trends Genet. 21, 227–233 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d13290e705">2</a></sup>. However, the chalky and soft endosperm texture of <i>o2</i> impacts the transport and storage of the grain, and facilitates fungal infection and insect susceptibility<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Gibbon, B. C. &amp; Larkins, B. A. Molecular genetic approaches to developing quality protein maize. Trends Genet. 21, 227–233 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d13290e712">2</a></sup>. To circumvent these problems, a long-term effort by plant breeders restored kernel hardness in the <i>o2</i> mutant via the introduction of quantitative trait loci (QTLs) known as <i>o2 modifier</i>s (<i>Mo2</i>s), creating quality protein maize (QPM)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Gibbon, B. C. &amp; Larkins, B. A. Molecular genetic approaches to developing quality protein maize. Trends Genet. 21, 227–233 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d13290e726">2</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#Fig1">1</a>). QPM is now produced in many developing countries, where people consume maize as a regular protein source.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Segregation of endosperm modification in QPM Pool 42."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Segregation of endosperm modification in QPM Pool 42.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-14023-2/MediaObjects/41467_2019_14023_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-14023-2/MediaObjects/41467_2019_14023_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="511" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Because QPM Pool 42 is not homozygous for <i>o2</i> modifiers, it segregates progeny ears and kernels with varying degrees of modification. From left to right, ear 1 is totally unmodified and therefore all kernels are opaque; ear 2 is partially modified and only a few kernels are vitreous; ear 3 is mostly modified, with sporadic kernels that are mosaics of virtuousness and opaque endosperm; ear 4 is almost completely modified. The asterisk, arrow and arrowhead in ears 2 and 3 indicate kernels with vitreous, mosaic and opaque phenotypes. Scale bar = 10 mm.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>There is good evidence that the vitreous kernel phenotype of QPM is determined by several unlinked loci<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Holding, D. R. et al. Genetic analysis of opaque2 modifier loci in quality protein maize. Theor. Appl Genet. 117, 157–170 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d13290e748">3</a></sup>. The challenge for maize breeders was to simultaneously introduce multiple non-linked <i>Mo2</i>s from tropical germplasms into temperate inbred lines, while retaining a homozygous recessive <i>o2</i> locus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Holding, D. R. et al. Genetic analysis of opaque2 modifier loci in quality protein maize. Theor. Appl Genet. 117, 157–170 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d13290e758">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Holding, D. R. et al. Characterization of opaque2 modifier QTLs and candidate genes in recombinant inbred lines derived from the K0326Y quality protein maize inbred. Theor. Appl Genet. 122, 783–794 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d13290e761">4</a></sup>. It was shown that the 27-kD γ-zein (γ27), a cysteine-rich protein, is an important factor for endosperm modification in QPM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Wu, Y., Holding, D. R. &amp; Messing, J. Gamma-zeins are essential for endosperm modification in quality protein maize. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 107, 12810–12815 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d13290e765">5</a></sup>. This protein plays a role in the initiation and stabilization of zein protein bodies (PBs), allowing them to accumulate in the rough endoplasmic reticulum<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Wu, Y. &amp; Messing, J. RNA interference-mediated change in protein body morphology and seed opacity through loss of different zein proteins. Plant Physiol. 153, 337–347 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d13290e770">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Guo, X. et al. Nonredundant function of zeins and their correct stoichiometric ratio drive protein body formation in maize endosperm. Plant Physiol. 162, 1359–1369 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d13290e773">7</a></sup>. <i>qγ27</i>, a major <i>Mo2</i> derived from a gene duplication at the <i>γ27</i> locus, elevates both 27-kD γ-zein transcript and protein levels, leading to more PBs that that coalesce around starch granules; this creates vitreous endosperm during kernel desiccation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Holding, D. R. et al. Genetic analysis of opaque2 modifier loci in quality protein maize. Theor. Appl Genet. 117, 157–170 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d13290e786">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Wu, Y., Holding, D. R. &amp; Messing, J. Gamma-zeins are essential for endosperm modification in quality protein maize. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 107, 12810–12815 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d13290e789">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Liu, H. et al. Gene duplication confers enhanced expression of 27-kDa gamma-zein for endosperm modification in quality protein maize. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, 4964–4969 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d13290e792">8</a></sup>. However, other mechanisms contributing to how starchy <i>o2</i> endosperm is modified in QPM are not well understood.</p><p>To characterize additional <i>Mo2</i> loci, we sequence the genome of a QPM line K0326Y and compare it with B73 and Mo17 for genomic variations. We conduct a bulked segregation analysis of the F<sub>2</sub> population of K0326Y and W64A<i>o2</i> and identify <i>Mo2</i> QTLs. We describe a set of differentially expressed genes (DEGs) from RNA-seq analysis between QPMs and <i>o2</i> mutants. We emphasize a group of candidate genes tightly linked to <i>Mo2</i> loci, with genomic structural variations and altered expression in QPMs. Based on these and previous studies, we hypothesize that an increased generation of ATP through glycolysis and an enhanced unfolded protein response (UPR) can contribute to alleviating an energy deficiency in <i>o2</i>, and directly or indirectly facilitate the formation of vitreous endosperm in QPM endosperm. The high-quality QPM genome sequence and the underlying mechanism of vitreous endosperm development are useful for future molecular characterization and creation of QPMs.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome assembly and validation</h3><p>The genome of K0326Y was sequenced and assembled using three technologies: PacBio single-molecule real-time (SMRT) sequencing, Illumina paired-end sequencing and BioNano optical mapping (Methods). The initial genome of K0326Y was assembled into 2,148 Mb by using 28.35 million long reads with an N50 read length of 16.6 kb and about 139-fold coverage (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM1">1</a><a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM1">–3</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM1">1</a>), resulting in 1,221 contigs with an N50 of 6.99 Mb (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#Tab1">1</a>, Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM1">4</a>,  <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM1">5</a>), comparable to the recent published SK genome with a contig N50 of 5.93 Mb (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Yang, N. et al. Genome assembly of a tropical maize inbred line provides insights into structural variation and crop improvement. Nat. Genet. 51, 1052–1059 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d13290e853">9</a>)</sup>. Assembled contigs were corrected with 132.5 Gb PacBio consensus sequences and 217.5 Gb high-quality Illumina paired-end reads. These processed contigs were subjected to hybrid assembly by optical maps created from 389.3 Gb BioNano molecules. The assembly contained 870 scaffolds with an N50 scaffold size of 27.98 Mb (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#Tab1">1</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM1">5</a>). The B73 reference genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Improved maize reference genome with single-molecule technologies. Nature 546, 524–527 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d13290e864">10</a></sup> was used to map and orient scaffolds onto K0326Y chromosomes, which accounted for 97.74% (2,112 Mb) of the total assembled sequences (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM1">6</a>). The total assembly size of the K0326Y genome was 2,161 Mb, similar to the recently published B73 (2,106 Mb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Improved maize reference genome with single-molecule technologies. Nature 546, 524–527 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d13290e871">10</a></sup> and Mo17 (2,183 Mb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Sun, S. et al. Extensive intraspecific gene order and gene structural variations between Mo17 and other maize genomes. Nat. Genet. 50, 1289–1295 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d13290e876">11</a></sup>. However, K0326Y had only 438 gaps and a final N50 contig size of 7.77 Mb, providing 5-fold higher contiguous sequences than B73 (contig N50: 1.25 Mb) and Mo17 (contig N50: 1.47 Mb) (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#Tab1">1</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM1">2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Global statistics for the K0326Y genome assembly.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To evaluate the quality of the assembled K0326Y genome, a high-density maize pan-genome genetic map containing ~4.4 million genotype-by-sequencing (GBS) tags<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Lu, F. et al. High-resolution genetic mapping of maize pan-genome sequence anchors. Nat. Commun. 6, 6914 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d13290e980">12</a></sup> was used. The alignment of the K0326Y genome with anchored GBS tags showed high consistency with respect to the position and orientation of the mapped scaffolds (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM1">3</a>). The assembly accuracy and completeness were supported by 100-fold Illumina reads with 93.8% mapping rate (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM1">2</a>). Approximately 95.8% (1380 out of 1440 genes) of embryophyta genes were detected in the K0326Y assembly according to BUSCO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Simao, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210–3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d13290e990">13</a></sup>, a percentage similar to that for B73 (96.1%) and Mo17 (95.4%) genome (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM1">7</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Repeat analysis</h3><p>Uninterrupted sequences represent a major improvement for chromosomal regions with high contents of repeat sequences<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Improved maize reference genome with single-molecule technologies. Nature 546, 524–527 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1005">10</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#Fig2">2</a>). A total of 83.32% of the K0326Y genome consisted of repetitive elements, including retrotransposons (77.38%), DNA transposons (4.72%), and unclassified elements (0.49%) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM1">8</a>). From retrotransposons, 136,191 high-confidence intact long terminal repeats (LTRs) were identified within the 10 chromosomes, slightly more than that reported for B73 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Improved maize reference genome with single-molecule technologies. Nature 546, 524–527 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1015">10</a></sup>). Families of <i>Gypsy</i> and <i>Copia</i> retrotransposons represented ~43.44% and 23.74%, respectively, of the K0326Y assembled sequences. The overall composition of the retrotransposon families in K0326Y was very similar to that in the Mo17 and B73 genomes, as they occurred before domestication (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM1">4</a>). Still, a few families showed copy number variations among K0326Y, B73, and Mo17, such as the LTR family of <i>Ty1</i>/<i>Copia RLC_ebel</i>, with five copies in B73, but 159 and 154 copies in K0326Y and Mo17. There were 647 copies of <i>Ty3/Gypsy RLG_huck_</i>AC214833 in B73, compared with 482 and 446 copies in K0326Y and Mo17, respectively. A more similar pattern of LTR family copy numbers was observed in K03236Y and Mo17, compared to K03236Y and B73. The centromeric regions for each chromosome were reconstructed by analysis of centromere-related long terminal repeat (CRM) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM1">9</a>) and 156-bp tandem repeats (CentC)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Zhong, C. X. et al. Centromeric retroelements and satellites interact with maize kinetochore protein CENH3. Plant Cell 14, 2825–2836 (2002)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1042">14</a></sup> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM1">10</a>), showing a similar distribution pattern like B73 (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM1">5,</a> <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM1">6</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Genomic landscape of the K0326Y genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Genomic landscape of the K0326Y genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-14023-2/MediaObjects/41467_2019_14023_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-14023-2/MediaObjects/41467_2019_14023_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="685" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b> Transposable-element density. <b>b</b> gene density. <b>c</b> Gene expression levels. <b>d</b> SNPs. <b>e</b> Indels. <b>f</b> PAV distribution. For the tracks of <b>d</b>, <b>e</b>, and f, the outer layer is B73 and the inner is Mo17. The sliding window is 1 Mb for all tracks. Source data are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Genome annotation</h3><p>A total of 1,618,691 high-quality full-length non-chimeric reads (HQ-FLNC), i.e., full-length cDNA consensus sequences, were identified from PacBio isoform sequencing, generating 247,616 non-redundant transcripts to predict gene models using marker-P<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Improved maize reference genome with single-molecule technologies. Nature 546, 524–527 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1096">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Campbell, M. S. et al. MAKER-P: a tool kit for the rapid creation, management, and quality control of plant genome annotations. Plant Physiol. 164, 513–524 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1099">15</a></sup> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM1">11</a>). A set of 38,238 genes with 60,475 transcripts was identified in the K0326Y genome (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM1">11</a>), comparable to B73 (39,200 genes)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Improved maize reference genome with single-molecule technologies. Nature 546, 524–527 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1109">10</a></sup> and Mo17 (38,620 genes)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Sun, S. et al. Extensive intraspecific gene order and gene structural variations between Mo17 and other maize genomes. Nat. Genet. 50, 1289–1295 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1113">11</a></sup>. The full-length cDNA data improved the K0326Y genome annotation by the fact that 69% of gene models were supported by full-length transcripts (CDS coverage &gt; 50%) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM1">12</a>). In addition, 37,861 (99.01%) K0326Y gene models could be assigned to specific chromosomal locations.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Comparative genomics</h3><p>Understanding the intraspecific variation of maize has important implications for crop improvement and plant breeding. Given the contiguous genome sequences, we were able to investigate structural diversity between the tropical K0326Y and two temperate inbreds, B73 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Improved maize reference genome with single-molecule technologies. Nature 546, 524–527 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1129">10</a></sup>) and Mo17 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Sun, S. et al. Extensive intraspecific gene order and gene structural variations between Mo17 and other maize genomes. Nat. Genet. 50, 1289–1295 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1133">11</a>)</sup>. Approximately 58% of K0326Y genome sequences matched one-to-one syntenic blocks of B73 and Mo17, respectively (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM1">6</a>). The genome-wide proportion of syntenic regions between K0326Y and B73 or Mo17 was lower than a recent analysis between B73 and Mo17 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Sun, S. et al. Extensive intraspecific gene order and gene structural variations between Mo17 and other maize genomes. Nat. Genet. 50, 1289–1295 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1141">11</a></sup>), possibly due to the intensified divergent selections of seed yield and reproductive success between B73 and Mo17, which is important for heterosis between the two heterotic groups<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Reif, J. C., Hallauer, A. R. &amp; Melchinger, A. E. Heterosis and heterotic patterns in maize. Maydica 50, 215–223 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1145">16</a></sup>. There were two inversions found to be specific for K0326Y compared to temperate and tropical maize lines. Chromosome 1 harbored one large pericentric inversion of 8.5 Mb, which was supported by the BioNano optical map with their breakpoints (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM1">6,</a> <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM1">7</a>). The inversion was different, with one of 1.7-Mb of the SK Chromosome 1 that originated from South America, indicating that this structural variation could be specific for K0326Y<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Yang, N. et al. Genome assembly of a tropical maize inbred line provides insights into structural variation and crop improvement. Nat. Genet. 51, 1052–1059 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1156">9</a></sup>. It was reported that some genes in the inverted region exhibited a significant association with maize flowering time<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Romero Navarro, J. A. et al. A study of allelic diversity underlying flowering-time adaptation in maize landraces. Nat. Genet. 49, 476–480 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1160">17</a></sup>. Another large paracentric inversion of 5.8 Mb was located at the centromere of chromosome 4 (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM1">6</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM1">7</a>). Most genes in the inversion have functions related to carbohydrate metabolism and gene regulation. K0326Y is a QPM line carrying the <i>o2</i> mutation. Comparison of the <i>O2</i> gene in K0326Y with B73 and Mo17 indicated there was a 4958-bp <i>rbg</i> transposon insertion 249-bp upstream of the start codon (ATG), similar to other <i>o2</i> alleles (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM1">8</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Chen, Y. et al. Transposable element rbg induces the differential expression of opaque-2 mutant gene in two maize o2 NILs derived from the same inbred line. PLoS ONE 9, e85159 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1186">18</a></sup>. As expected, gene expression data showed that transcription of <i>O2</i> was inhibited due to the <i>rbg</i> insertion.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Genomic polymorphism and structural variations</h3><p>The maize genome exhibits very high levels of genetic diversity with respect to SNPs, small InDels, and structural variations, which contribute to phenotypic diversity and heterosis in maize hybrids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Springer, N. M. et al. Maize inbreds exhibit high levels of copy number variation (CNV) and presence/absence variation (PAV) in genome content. PLoS Genet .5, e1000734 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1206">19</a></sup>. We identified a total of 10,205,511 SNPs and 1,397,901 InDels (&lt;100 bp) between K0326Y and B73, with an average of 8.35 SNPs and 1.14 InDels per kilobase. There were 9655,364 SNPs and 1,458,329 InDels (&lt;100 bp) between K0326Y and Mo17, with an average of 7.77 SNPs and 1.17 InDels per kilobase (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM1">13</a>). The genetic polymorphism affects 8,702 genes in B73 and 6,009 genes in Mo17, including frameshift, stop-codon loss and stop-codon acquisition (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM1">9</a>), which could contribute to other functions in K0326Y. We identified 19,778 insertions (&gt;100 bp) and 39,931 deletions between K0326Y and B73 that could affect 6,538 and 10,463 genes, respectively. In the case of K0326Y and Mo17, 32,071 insertions could affect 9,323 genes and 46,381 deletions were found in 12,456 genes, respectively (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM1">14</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM1">15</a>).</p><p>The distribution of presence/absence variations (PAVs) that were only present in K0326Y but entirely missing in B73 and Mo17 was identified. A total of 39,479 segments were identified in K0326Y with a total length of 154.7 Mb absent in B73. Similarly, there were 37,906 segments in K0326Y with a total length of 149.5 Mb missing in Mo17 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#Fig2">2</a>). These PAV regions affected 3568 genes in K0326Y (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM5">1</a>). The expression of 631 PAV genes was upregulated based on comparison of RNA-seq data from 16-DAP developing endosperm of K0326Y QPM compared to W64A<i>o2</i>. These genes were enriched for pathways like starch biosynthesis and metabolic processes, ATPase activity, auxin biosynthesis and sulfur transmembrane activity (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM1">10</a>). Interestingly, it was found that the PAV genes in B73, Mo17, and PH207 are also present in wild maize relatives, and occurred before the completion of maize domestication, but after the divergence of sorghum and maize<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Sun, S. et al. Extensive intraspecific gene order and gene structural variations between Mo17 and other maize genomes. Nat. Genet. 50, 1289–1295 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1237">11</a></sup>.</p><p>Gene duplications provide a mechanism to change phenotypes via a gene dosage effect or a gene function from divergence. We found that the number of singleton genes in B73, Mo17, and K0326Y were quite conserved, whereas K0326Y had fewer segmental duplications (12,259), but more dispersed duplicated genes (16,777) compared to B73 and Mo17 (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM1">16</a>). Tandemly duplicated genes were located in proximal neighborhoods and potentially shared the same regulatory elements. We identified 1261 tandem gene copy clusters, accounting for 3842 annotated genes in the K0326Y genome (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM1">16</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Genetic mapping of <i>Mo2</i>s</h3><p>Given the ubiquitous genetic variability between QPM and other <i>o2</i> mutants, it was expected that certain variations, including the <i>Mo2</i> genes, are responsible for their phenotypic differences. To map chromosomal regions linked with endosperm modification, we followed the methods of Holding<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Holding, D. R. et al. Genetic analysis of opaque2 modifier loci in quality protein maize. Theor. Appl Genet. 117, 157–170 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1268">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Holding, D. R. et al. Characterization of opaque2 modifier QTLs and candidate genes in recombinant inbred lines derived from the K0326Y quality protein maize inbred. Theor. Appl Genet. 122, 783–794 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1271">4</a></sup> and crossed K0326Y with W64A<i>o2</i>. The pooled DNA from leaf tissue of F<sub>2</sub> vitreous and opaque kernels was used for a bulked segregation analysis, with next-generation sequencing (BSA-seq) to identify QTLs associated with endosperm modification. To avoid a masking effect by the major <i>Mo2</i> (<i>qγ27</i>), we selected vitreous and opaque kernels with two copies of <i>γ27</i> gene at the <i>γ27</i> locus for QTL-seq analysis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Liu, H. et al. Gene duplication confers enhanced expression of 27-kDa gamma-zein for endosperm modification in quality protein maize. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, 4964–4969 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1293">8</a></sup> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM1">11</a>). Three <i>Mo2</i> QTLs were located on chromosomes 1, 7, and 9 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#Fig3">3b</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM1">12</a>), consistent with previous mapping data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Holding, D. R. et al. Genetic analysis of opaque2 modifier loci in quality protein maize. Theor. Appl Genet. 117, 157–170 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1310">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Holding, D. R. et al. Characterization of opaque2 modifier QTLs and candidate genes in recombinant inbred lines derived from the K0326Y quality protein maize inbred. Theor. Appl Genet. 122, 783–794 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1313">4</a></sup>. Another QTL with a sharp peak was found at a region on chromosome 6, with a large K0326Y-specific fragment insertion (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM1">3</a>). Whether it is a false-positive or not needs further examination, because crossovers in a F<sub>2</sub> population are very limited.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Mapping of o2 modifiers."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Mapping of <i>o2</i> modifiers.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-14023-2/MediaObjects/41467_2019_14023_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-14023-2/MediaObjects/41467_2019_14023_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="474" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b> Construction of F<sub>2</sub> population by using K0326Y(QPM) and W64A<i>o2</i> (<i>o2</i>). K0326Y has a vitreous phenotype and W64A<i>o2</i> has an opaque phenotype. <b>b</b> mapping o2 modifiers by bulk segregant analysis (BSA) of a segregating population derived from a K0326Y and W64A<i>o2</i> cross. The G’ value is a smoothed version of the standard G statistic in each 4-Mb sliding window. Green line indicates threshold of G’ value corresponding to an FDR of 8 × 10<sup>−7</sup>. Scale bar = 5 mm.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To identify candidate genes corresponding to <i>Mo2</i> QTLs, total RNA from developing maize endosperm was collected at 16 DAP from vitreous and opaque kernels. The transcriptomic profiles were built to compare gene expression levels between K0326Y and W64A<i>o2</i> and between CM105<i>Mo2</i> (another QPM line in the CM105 background) and CM105<i>o2</i>. There were 1791 DEGs that overlapped in the two comparisons, of which 926 were upregulated and 865 downregulated (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM6">Supplementary Data 2</a>). The increased DEGs were significantly enriched in the terms of chaperone binding, unfolded protein binding, protein folding, and response to heat and temperature stimulus (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM1">13</a>). Notably, 43 genes encoding heat shock protein (HSPs) and HSP transcription factors were significantly upregulated in the QPM (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM7">Supplementary Data 3</a>). As previously suggested<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Guo, X. M. et al. Pyrophosphate-dependent fructose-6-phosphate 1-phosphotransferase induction and attenuation of Hsp gene expression during endosperm modification in Quality Protein Maize. Plant Physiol. 158, 917–929 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1383">20</a></sup>, they might be activated and reduce stress effects by reassembling unfolded or aggregated proteins created by the <i>o2</i> mutation.</p><p>There were 216 (117 upregulated and 99 downregulated) DEGs that cross-checked with genes from the QTL-seq analysis, narrowing the pool of candidate genes contributing to a vitreous kernel phenotype and environmental adaption (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM8">Supplementary Data 4</a>). After sorting the 216 gene list, there were 125 containing SNPs, 44 with insertions (&gt;100 bp), and 43 with deletions (&gt;100 bp), compared to B73, as well as 18 exhibiting tandem duplications, and 19 belonging to PAVs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM8">Supplementary Data 4</a>). Still, most DEGs that could not be mapped to QTLs might not necessarily be causative of the QPM phenotype, but they can provide clues as to an effect of Mo2s that set off a signaling cascade, leading to formation of vitreous endosperm. They could also be under regulation of Mo2s, reflecting downstream effects of changes in kernel composition.</p><p>As we integrated genome variations to identify candidate genes associated with <i>Mo2</i>s that deserve further study, those with allelic variations, elevated expression, and located within QTL regions became apparent. On chromosome 1, there was 85-bp deleted from the <i>O10</i> promoter in K0326Y, which was highly expressed in K0326Y and CM105<i>Mo2</i> QPM lines<i>. O10</i> encodes a cereal-specific protein that regulates zein deposition and organization in PBs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Yao, D. et al. Maize opaque10 encodes a cereal-specific protein that is essential for the proper distribution of zeins in endosperm protein bodies. PLoS Genet. 12, e1006270 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1414">21</a></sup>. Mutations in <i>O10</i> create abnormal distribution of zeins in PBs. Whether upregulation of <i>O10</i> functions as a <i>Mo2</i> or is required for <i>Mo2</i>-mediated endosperm modification remains to be investigated.</p><p>Tandem duplication of the <i>γ27</i> locus (<i>qγ27</i>), which was previously designated the <i>Standard</i> allele (<i>S</i>) for bearing two copies of <i>γ27</i> gene, is a major <i>Mo2</i> for endosperm modification in QPM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Liu, H. et al. Gene duplication confers enhanced expression of 27-kDa gamma-zein for endosperm modification in quality protein maize. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, 4964–4969 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1454">8</a></sup>. Besides <i>γ27</i>, this duplication involves three other genes (GRMZM2G565441, GRMZM2G138976, and GRMZM5G873335) based on B73_vs3, which were merged into one gene with fifteen exons encoding ARID-transcription factor 4 (Zm00001d020593)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Improved maize reference genome with single-molecule technologies. Nature 546, 524–527 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1461">10</a></sup> in B73_vs4 and K0326Y. Although all QPM lines have this duplication, a contiguous genomic sequence was not previously available and its variation was unknown<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Liu, H. et al. Gene duplication confers enhanced expression of 27-kDa gamma-zein for endosperm modification in quality protein maize. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, 4964–4969 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1465">8</a></sup>. Here, we were able to expand this sequence to 28 kb, totally covering the duplication (~15 kb). The duplicated fragments have many SNPs that could distinguish them. The first copy of <i>ARID4</i> has a 1923-bp deletion in its 3’ end, resulting in four missing exons, compared to the second gene copy (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM1">14</a>). A polymorphic PCR primer pair flanking this deletion could be designed to select for the duplication during QPM breeding, depending on the presence of two PCR bands. A global alignment showed that the <i>qγ27</i> locus between Mo17 and K0326Y were nearly identical. The B73-<i>γ27</i> allele was closer to the first fragment copy in K0326Y, and B73-<i>ARID4</i> was closer to the second one in K0326Y (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM1">15</a>). This indicates that the single-copy <i>γ27</i> allele in B73 could have been produced from DNA rearrangement of the duplicated allele. Therefore, modern maize lines might have had at least three types of single-copy <i>γ27</i> allele, whereas the tandem duplication might have occurred early before maize domestication (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM1">16</a>).</p><p>In agreement with previous studies, our analysis of QTL mapping (BSA-seq) and gene expression (RNA-seq) identified another promising <i>Mo2</i> candidate gene on chromosome 9, <i>Pfpα</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Holding, D. R. et al. Genetic analysis of opaque2 modifier loci in quality protein maize. Theor. Appl Genet. 117, 157–170 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1506">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Holding, D. R. et al. Characterization of opaque2 modifier QTLs and candidate genes in recombinant inbred lines derived from the K0326Y quality protein maize inbred. Theor. Appl Genet. 122, 783–794 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1509">4</a></sup>. <i>Pfpα</i> encodes the α-regulatory subunit of pyrophosphate-dependent fructose-6-phosphate 1-phosphotransferase and serves as a non-ATP-requiring enzyme during glycolysis. Transcript and protein levels, as well as enzyme activity of <i>Pfpα</i> were greatly increased in QPM endosperms relative to normal and <i>o2</i> mutants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Holding, D. R. et al. Genetic analysis of opaque2 modifier loci in quality protein maize. Theor. Appl Genet. 117, 157–170 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1523">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Holding, D. R. et al. Characterization of opaque2 modifier QTLs and candidate genes in recombinant inbred lines derived from the K0326Y quality protein maize inbred. Theor. Appl Genet. 122, 783–794 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1526">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Guo, X. M. et al. Pyrophosphate-dependent fructose-6-phosphate 1-phosphotransferase induction and attenuation of Hsp gene expression during endosperm modification in Quality Protein Maize. Plant Physiol. 158, 917–929 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1529">20</a></sup>. As noted, this could lead to increased glycolytic flux in QPM endosperm and ameliorate an aspect of the <i>o2</i> phenotype including a limitation in ATP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Guo, X. M. et al. Pyrophosphate-dependent fructose-6-phosphate 1-phosphotransferase induction and attenuation of Hsp gene expression during endosperm modification in Quality Protein Maize. Plant Physiol. 158, 917–929 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1536">20</a></sup>. The identity of the causative polymorphisms and underlying expression mechanisms influencing <i>Pfpα</i> had not been elucidated due to the lack of a QPM genome sequence. We found that the <i>Pfpα</i> locus in K0326Y showed dramatic structural variations compared to Mo17 and B73. In K0326Y<i>-Pfpα</i>, there was a 983-bp <i>Helitron</i> in the promotor and a 2485-bp insertion in the second intron, compared to Mo17-<i>Pfpα</i> and B73<i>-Pfpα</i>. However, the Mo17-<i>Pfpα</i> and B73<i>-Pfpα</i> both contained a 6181-bp CACTA, a 10,685-bp retrotransposon in the second intron, and a 6037-bp retrotransposon in the 13th intron, compared to the K0326Y<i>-Pfpα</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#Fig4">4a</a>). To investigate whether these insertions and deletions affect <i>Pfpα</i> expression, the transcripts of each exon were normalized to the exon size and sequencing depth. We found that the transcript abundance of the whole gene and each exon of <i>Pfpα</i> in QPMs (K0326Y and CM105<i>Mo2</i>) were higher than non-QPM lines (W64A<i>o2</i>, CM105<i>o2</i>, CM105+) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#Fig4">4b</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM1">17</a>). In addition, we found that 65% of QPM lines have the <i>Helitron</i> insertion. The <i>Helitron</i> insertion was retained in 95% of the vitreous endosperm kernels of the segregating F<sub>2</sub> population (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#Fig4">4c</a>), and is therefore clearly associated with the vitreous kernel phenotype. Mobile genetic elements can drive genome evolution, and also alter gene expression by insertion within introns, exons or regulatory region. Transposon-mediated mutations in the promoter of <i>Adh1</i> resulted in a significant increase of gene expression in pollen<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Dawe, R. K., Lachmansingh, A. R. &amp; Freeling, M. Transposon-mediated mutations in the untranslated leader of maize Adh1 that increase and decrease pollen-specific gene expression. Plant Cell 5, 311–319 (1993)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1609">22</a></sup>, and a transposable element inserted into the regulatory region of a maize domestication gene (<i>teosinte branched1</i>, <i>tb1</i>) acted as an enhancer of its expression, which partially explained increased apical dominance in maize<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Studer, A., Zhao, Q., Ross-Ibarra, J. &amp; Doebley, J. Identification of a functional transposon insertion in the maize domestication gene tb1. Nat. Genet. 43, 1160–1163 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1619">23</a></sup>. The <i>Helitron</i> insertion into the promoter of <i>Pfpα</i> might be partially causative of endosperm modification in QPM. Whether other transposable elements influence functional variation requires further research.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Pfpα associated with o2 endosperm modification."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: <i>Pfpα</i> associated with <i>o2</i> endosperm modification.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-14023-2/MediaObjects/41467_2019_14023_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-14023-2/MediaObjects/41467_2019_14023_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="570" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>a</b> Structural variation of <i>Pfpα</i> among K0326Y, Mo17, and B73. The gene structure of <i>Pfpα</i> is shown with exons in blue boxes and introns with a black line. The <i>Helitron</i> in the promoter is labelled as a green bar. The LTR retrotransposon insertions, abbreviated with LTR, are illustrated by orange inverted triangles. Other insertions are represented by purple inverted triangles. The physical location is based on the K0326Y genome. (<b>b</b>) Relative gene expression of <i>Pfpα</i> for wild-type (CM105+), <i>o2</i> (CM105<i>o2</i> and W64A<i>o2</i>), and QPM (CM105M<i>o2</i> and K0326Y). <i>P</i>-values are determined by two-tailed Student’s <i>t</i>-test. The error bars are defined as sample standard deviation from the sample size (biologically independent samples) of <i>n</i> = 5. <b>c</b> Distribution of <i>Helitron</i> insertions in QPM inbred lines, GWAS population and K0326Y X W64A<i>o2</i> F<sub>2</sub> segregation population; <i>x</i>-axis shows the maize populations and the <i>y</i>-axis is the linked percentage. Source data underlying Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM9">4b, c</a> are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Among the <i>Mo2</i> candidates, two genes, <i>SR45a</i> and <i>ERDJ3A</i>, were extremely close to the peak on chromosome 9 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM8">Supplementary Data 4</a>). The level of the <i>SR45a</i> transcripts increased 2–28 times in QPM compared with <i>o2</i> mutants (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM1">18</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM8">Supplementary Data 4</a>). This gene was responsible for RNA splicing, and was also elevated when Arabidopsis was under stress<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Tanabe, N., Yoshimura, K., Kimura, A., Yabuta, Y. &amp; Shigeoka, S. Differential expression of alternatively spliced mRNAs of Arabidopsis SR protein homologs, atSR30 and atSR45a, in response to environmental stress. Plant Cell Physiol. 48, 1036–1049 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1734">24</a></sup>. Interestingly, there was a 399-bp DNA transposon of the <i>hAT</i> element in the 5th intron of <i>SR45a</i> compared with B73 and Mo17, and this region, with an associated index of 61% (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM1">18</a>), was highly linked to the vitreous trait in the F<sub>2</sub> population. It was reported that ERDJ3A is a co-chaperone with heat shock proteins (HSPs) expressed in plant cells. The gene could be greatly induced under ER stress in rice and facilitates the delivery of unfolded proteins between vacuoles and ER<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Ohta, M. &amp; Takaiwa, F. Emerging features of ER resident J-proteins in plants. Plant Signal Behav. 9, e28194 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1750">25</a></sup>. We found its expression was elevated 5–8 times in QPM over <i>o2</i>, and that a 26,022-bp retrotransposon was located downstream of <i>ERDJ3A</i> DNA sequences (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM1">19</a>), which might regulate gene expression due to its large size and spatial change. Whether the insertion affects gene expression and in turn plays a role in endosperm modification remains to be further investigated.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><p>Contiguous sequence information achieved by shotgun DNA sequencing and sequencing technologies advances the molecular genetic analysis of complex genomes in plants and animals. The B73 maize genome was improved with a 52-fold increase in contig length and a notable retrieval of intergenic spaces and centromeres by using single-molecule technologies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Improved maize reference genome with single-molecule technologies. Nature 546, 524–527 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1772">10</a></sup>. Long-read sequences not only uncover extensive SNP and InDel variations, but also identify extensive structural variations between maize inbred lines, which is not apparent from genomes assembled with short reads. The tropical SK maize genome generated from long reads presents 80,614 polymorphic structural variants, across 521 diverse lines and serves as a reference genome to mine genetic variation for crop improvement<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Yang, N. et al. Genome assembly of a tropical maize inbred line provides insights into structural variation and crop improvement. Nat. Genet. 51, 1052–1059 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1776">9</a></sup>. Extended contiguous chromosomal information also provides an approach to reconstruct breeding histories. The high-quality Mo17 genome sequence was found to contain extensive intraspecific genome diversity compared to other maize lines, which provided the opportunity to explore the molecular basis of heterosis and genome evolution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Sun, S. et al. Extensive intraspecific gene order and gene structural variations between Mo17 and other maize genomes. Nat. Genet. 50, 1289–1295 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1780">11</a></sup>. In particular, traits composed of multiple QTLs with different weights can be assigned to precise chromosomal regions.</p><p>Analysis of maize genome intraspecific variation has important implications for crop improvement and plant breeding.
