Sequencing an entire genome allows us to reveal the degree of copy number variation and presence/absence variation in maize, which is frequently associated with a change in gene expression and contributes to extraordinary phenotypic diversity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Springer, N. M. et al. Maize inbreds exhibit high levels of copy number variation (CNV) and presence/absence variation (PAV) in genome content. PLoS Genet .5, e1000734 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1787">19</a></sup>. In this study, we identified a high level of gene duplications in the K0326Y genome, indicating potential impact on phenotypic diversity and underlying mechanism of heterosis. There are more than 40 gene copies accounting for the abundant α-zeins that comprise the majority of seed storage proteins in maize<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Dong, J. et al. Candidate gene identification of existing or induced mutations with pipelines applicable to large genomes. Plant J. 97, 673–682 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1791">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Xu, J. H. &amp; Messing, J. Organization of the prolamin gene family provides insight into the evolution of the maize genome and gene duplications in grass species. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 105, 14330–14335 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1794">27</a></sup>. The tandem duplications of these genes may be important for breeding, as the expansion and contraction of gene copies could be tied to important quantitative traits. It was shown that a gene duplication conferred enhanced transcript and protein levels of the <i>γ27</i> gene, which was proved important for endosperm modification in QPM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Liu, H. et al. Gene duplication confers enhanced expression of 27-kDa gamma-zein for endosperm modification in quality protein maize. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, 4964–4969 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1801">8</a></sup>. The availability of the whole genome allowed us to investigate this and other loci and to make comparative analyses between QPM and non-QPM genotypes. It is possible that knowledge of genomic variation in maize will provide source material to generate alleles. There is evidence that strong artificial selection on specific anthocyanin coloration patterns has often led to the formation of complex alleles with tandem duplications<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Walker, E. L., Robbins, T. P., Bureau, T. E., Kermicle, J. &amp; Dellaporta, S. L. Transposon-mediated chromosomal rearrangements and gene duplications in the formation of the maize R-r complex. EMBO J. 14, 2350–2363 (1995)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1805">28</a></sup>.</p><p>Previous molecular characterization of K0326Y QPM was limited by the absence of a complete genome sequence. The <i>qγ27</i> QTL was associated with a major <i>Mo2</i> based on linkage mapping<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Campbell, M. S. et al. MAKER-P: a tool kit for the rapid creation, management, and quality control of plant genome annotations. Plant Physiol. 164, 513–524 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1818">15</a></sup>. To investigate the structure of <i>qγ27</i>, a Mo17 BAC clone containing the duplicated <i>γ27</i> gene was sequenced<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Liu, H. et al. Gene duplication confers enhanced expression of 27-kDa gamma-zein for endosperm modification in quality protein maize. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, 4964–4969 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1829">8</a></sup>, as it would have been extraordinary labor-intensive and time-consuming to determine the structure and expression of any <i>Mo2</i> candidate gene in K0326Y without the genome sequence. In this study, we produced a high-quality QPM genome sequence with a contig N50 of 6.99 Mb and captured many sequence polymorphisms and structural variations. We can now trace different introgressions achieved through intensive breeding and thereby uncover multiple potential <i>Mo2</i> molecular mechanisms for the development of hard kernel varieties, which can accelerate future breeding via marker-assisted selection.</p><p>Holding et al. mapped three major <i>Mo2</i> QTLs in K0326Y on chromosomes 1, 7, and 9, using two independently developed QPM lines and recombinant inbred lines (RILs)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Holding, D. R. et al. Genetic analysis of opaque2 modifier loci in quality protein maize. Theor. Appl Genet. 117, 157–170 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1845">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Holding, D. R. et al. Characterization of opaque2 modifier QTLs and candidate genes in recombinant inbred lines derived from the K0326Y quality protein maize inbred. Theor. Appl Genet. 122, 783–794 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1848">4</a></sup>. A microarray hybridization identified 16 differentially upregulated genes in QPM, including <i>Pfpα</i> and <i>γ27</i>, some of which showed a consistent expression pattern with an increase in vitreous endosperm compared with opaque RILs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Holding, D. R. et al. Genetic analysis of opaque2 modifier loci in quality protein maize. Theor. Appl Genet. 117, 157–170 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1858">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Holding, D. R. et al. Characterization of opaque2 modifier QTLs and candidate genes in recombinant inbred lines derived from the K0326Y quality protein maize inbred. Theor. Appl Genet. 122, 783–794 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1861">4</a></sup>. The PFP protein complex, consisting of two regulatory <i>α</i>-subunits and two catalytic β-subunits, is a non-ATP-dependent enzyme that could catalyze a rate-limiting step in glycolysis. It was proposed that increased PFP<i>α</i> activity in QPM could attenuate oxygen stress in the endosperm by increasing diminished ATP reserves; PFPβ expression was totally uniform between genotypes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Guo, X. M. et al. Pyrophosphate-dependent fructose-6-phosphate 1-phosphotransferase induction and attenuation of Hsp gene expression during endosperm modification in Quality Protein Maize. Plant Physiol. 158, 917–929 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1872">20</a></sup>. The O2 transcription factor could positively regulated pyruvate orthophosphate dikinase 1 (PPDK1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Liu, H. et al. Gene duplication confers enhanced expression of 27-kDa gamma-zein for endosperm modification in quality protein maize. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, 4964–4969 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1876">8</a></sup>, as this enzyme is downregulated in <i>o2</i> mutants, making it important during late-stage kernel filling. However, an exclusively cytosolic version of PPDK2 that might enhance non-ATP-dependent glycolytic flux was shown to be upregulated in QPM endosperm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Guo, X. M. et al. Pyrophosphate-dependent fructose-6-phosphate 1-phosphotransferase induction and attenuation of Hsp gene expression during endosperm modification in Quality Protein Maize. Plant Physiol. 158, 917–929 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1883">20</a></sup>. We identified another ATP-independent enzyme, cytosolic enolase (ENO), with increased gene expression in QPM. ENO is an integral enzyme in glycolysis, catalyzing the interconversion of 2-phosphoglycerate to PEP. It was reported that enolase transcript levels and enzyme activity were induced by anaerobic stress in maize<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Lal, S. K., Lee, C. &amp; Sachs, M. M. Differential regulation of enolase during anaerobiosis in maize. Plant Physiol. 118, 1285–1293 (1998)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1888">29</a></sup>. This is consistent with a hypothesis that the increased synthesis of non-ATP-requiring enzymes for glycolysis in QPM provides an energy-generating mechanism repressed in <i>o2</i>. This mechanism mollifies an energy deficiency, consistent with the previous assumption<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Guo, X. M. et al. Pyrophosphate-dependent fructose-6-phosphate 1-phosphotransferase induction and attenuation of Hsp gene expression during endosperm modification in Quality Protein Maize. Plant Physiol. 158, 917–929 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1895">20</a></sup>. RILs generated after seven generations of self-pollination from a cross between K0326Y and W64A<i>o2</i> (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Reif, J. C., Hallauer, A. R. &amp; Melchinger, A. E. Heterosis and heterotic patterns in maize. Maydica 50, 215–223 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1902">16</a>)</sup>, together with the K0326Y genome sequence, will be valuable to evaluate the contribution of individual <i>Mo2</i>s to QPM phenotype.</p><p>HSPs, a group of chaperones that assist in protein folding and assembly and maintain optimal conditions for plant growth and development<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Pegoraro, C., Mertz, L. M., da Maia, L. C., Rombaldi, C. V. &amp; de Oliveira, A. C. Importance of heat shock proteins in maize. J. Crop Sci. Biotechnol. 14, 85–95 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1914">30</a></sup>. Our RNA-seq experiments show that most <i>Hsp</i> genes are expressed at a higher level in QPM compared to <i>o2</i>, indicating that the <i>o2</i> mutant may not effectively activate the heat shock response to sustain protein folding. Still, some downregulated <i>Hsps</i> in QPM might have other roles due to functional divergence after maize tetraploidization. The effect on HSPs that we detected is reasonably consistent with earlier gene expression data that identified 8 <i>Hsps</i> among 37 downregulated genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Guo, X. M. et al. Pyrophosphate-dependent fructose-6-phosphate 1-phosphotransferase induction and attenuation of Hsp gene expression during endosperm modification in Quality Protein Maize. Plant Physiol. 158, 917–929 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1934">20</a></sup>, compared with 49 (43 increased and 6 decreased) <i>Hsp</i> genes in 1791 DGEs in this study. Our increased resolution of gene expression might result from the longer 125-bp paired-end reads<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Ozsolak, F. &amp; Milos, P. M. RNA sequencing: advances, challenges and opportunities. Nat. Rev. Genet. 12, 87–98 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1941">31</a></sup>, increasing the ability to distinguish homologous Hsp members in two sets of maize sub-genomes, whereas previous study had limited the power to define DGEs by using the shorter 51-bp single-end reads and lower sequence output generated from the early Illumina platform. The 43 of 49 increased HSPs indicates stronger UPRs in QPM. In rice, ERdj3A, which is located in protein storage vacuoles and induced under ER stress, is involved in protein folding and plant immunity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Ohta, M. &amp; Takaiwa, F. Emerging features of ER resident J-proteins in plants. Plant Signal Behav. 9, e28194 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1945">25</a></sup>. An elevated <i>ERDJ3A</i> in QPM could reflect physiological stress created by refolding proteins or facilitating ER-associated protein degradation.</p><p>Based on the previous studies of Holding et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Guo, X. M. et al. Pyrophosphate-dependent fructose-6-phosphate 1-phosphotransferase induction and attenuation of Hsp gene expression during endosperm modification in Quality Protein Maize. Plant Physiol. 158, 917–929 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1955">20</a></sup> and our findings, we propose a model for how starchy endosperm in <i>o2</i> is converted into a vitreous phenotype in QPM (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#Fig5">5</a>). During endosperm development, synthesis of zein proteins are stored in ER PBs, and their synthesis is influenced in a spatio-temporal pattern by ATP availability, which affects their numbers, sizes, and shapes. The inner maize endosperm is deficient in oxygen, and the glycolytic pathway, rather than the tricarboxylic acid (TCA) cycle and oxidative phosphorylation, becomes increasingly important to generate ATP (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM1">20</a>). When normal zein PBs, functional ER, and normal starch biosynthesis occur simultaneously in the presence of sufficient ATP, it facilitates development of a vitreous endosperm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Larkins, B. A., Wu, Y., Song, R. &amp; Messing, J. in Maize Kernel Development (ed. Larkins, B. A.) 175–189 (CABI, Oxfordshire, 2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1968">32</a></sup>. However, the <i>o2</i> mutant significantly reduces the amount of zeins and appears to impair normal protein folding in the ER. It was hypothesized that the activation of the unfolded protein response (UPR) in <i>o2</i> requires increased amounts of energy in form of ATP and increased levels of chaperons, including heat shock proteins<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Guo, X. M. et al. Pyrophosphate-dependent fructose-6-phosphate 1-phosphotransferase induction and attenuation of Hsp gene expression during endosperm modification in Quality Protein Maize. Plant Physiol. 158, 917–929 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1979">20</a></sup>. Moreover, the <i>o2</i> mutation has reduced expression of PPDK1, affecting glycolysis (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM1">20</a>) and aggravating the energy shortage<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Ozsolak, F. &amp; Milos, P. M. RNA sequencing: advances, challenges and opportunities. Nat. Rev. Genet. 12, 87–98 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1989">31</a></sup>. The <i>o2</i> mutation also reduces starch synthesis directly, resulting from transcriptional downregulation of genes involved in its synthesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Liu, H. et al. Gene duplication confers enhanced expression of 27-kDa gamma-zein for endosperm modification in quality protein maize. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, 4964–4969 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d13290e1997">8</a></sup>. These metabolic changes impact the way in which PBs and starch grains congeal during kernel desiccation, leading to opacity in the mature endosperm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Larkins, B. A., Wu, Y., Song, R. &amp; Messing, J. in Maize Kernel Development (ed. Larkins, B. A.) 175–189 (CABI, Oxfordshire, 2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2001">32</a></sup>. Alternatively, the pleiotropic effects of <i>o2</i> mutations could be explained by additive effects from other processes. The <i>o2</i> effect on PB structure through zein gene expression and ATP depletion, through <i>Pfpα</i> and <i>Ppdk1</i>, could be independent, but they combine to give a more severe endosperm phenotype than reducing zein proteins alone. The recovery of starch biosynthesis and the glycolysis pathway is supported by the increased expression of starch synthetic and glycolysis-related genes in QPM compared to <i>o2</i> maize (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM1">21</a>). This is consistent with multiple <i>Mo2</i>s involved in different metabolic pathways required to restore <i>o2</i> mutants to a vitreous phenotype (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#Fig5">5</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="A model illustrating QPM kernel development."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: A model illustrating QPM kernel development.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-14023-2/MediaObjects/41467_2019_14023_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-14023-2/MediaObjects/41467_2019_14023_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="211" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>The elevated gene expression is marked with a pink arrow, whereas the gene with decreased expression is labeled with a purple arrow. Solid lines represent direct or indirect connections. Not all details of pathway are shown in this diagram in order to simplify the model; more details are in the text. SS starch synthesis, PPDK pyruvate phosphate dikinase, ENO cytosolic enolase, FRK fructokinase, PGLS 6-phosphogluconolactonase, UPR unfolded protein response, ER endoplasmic reticulum, PFP phosphoenolpyruvate, HSPs heat shock proteins, ERDJ3A DnaJ protein.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Plant material</h3><p>The maize (<i>Zea mays</i>) inbred line K0326Y was selected for sequencing because of its previous role in the study of QPM. The seeds were obtained from the Holding laboratory at the University of Nebraska, Lincoln. For DNA extraction, plants were grown in a greenhouse at 25 °C for 12 days, then treated in darkness for 3 days to minimize chloroplast DNA; leaves of seedlings were harvested and frozen immediately in liquid nitrogen for extraction of DNA. Genomic DNA for library construction was obtained using the PacBio protocol<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Pacific, B. Preparing Arabidopsis Genomic DNA for Size-Selected ~20 kb SMRTbell™ Libraries. &#xA;https://www.pacb.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/Shared-Protocol-Preparing-Arabidopsis-DNA-for-20-kb-SMRTbell-Libraries.pdf&#xA;&#xA; (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2057">33</a></sup>. The quality of the DNA was assessed with Qubit, NanoDrop 2000, and pulsed-field electrophoresis.</p><p>To compare varying degrees of endosperm modification for <i>o2</i> mutants, 100-kernels of a QPM population, O2POOL 42, segregating <i>Mo2</i>s were obtained from the International Maize and Wheat Improvement Center, CIMMYT (<a href="http://mgb.cimmyt.org/gringlobal/accessiondetail.aspx?id=14778">http://mgb.cimmyt.org/gringlobal/accessiondetail.aspx?id = 14778</a>, plant ID 014785). They were grown in Sanya, Hainan.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">PacBio and Illumina library construction and sequencing</h3><p>Libraries for SMRT PacBio genome sequencing were constructed by using high molecular weight and high-quality DNA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Pacific, B. Preparing Arabidopsis Genomic DNA for Size-Selected ~20 kb SMRTbell™ Libraries. &#xA;https://www.pacb.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/Shared-Protocol-Preparing-Arabidopsis-DNA-for-20-kb-SMRTbell-Libraries.pdf&#xA;&#xA; (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2082">33</a></sup>. More than 10 μg of high-quality genomic DNA was sheared to ~20 -kb using g­TUBE (Covaris). Sheared genomic DNA was treated for damage repair and end repair, adaptor ligation, and size selection with a Blue Pippin system (Sage Science) to create SMRTbell template libraries. The final libraries were sequenced on the PacBio Sequel platform (Pacific Biosciences, CA, USA) using Sequel Sequencing Kit 2.1 Bundle. Libraries for Illumina PCR-free paired-end genome sequencing were constructed according to the manufacturer’s standard protocol (Illumina, CA, USA). Approximately 5 μg of genomic DNA was fragmented, then size-selected (350 bp) by agarose gel electrophoresis. The ends of selected DNA fragments were blunted with an A-base overhang and ligated to sequencing adapters. After quality control, all the PCR-free libraries were sequenced on an Illumina platform with a paired-end sequencing strategy.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">De novo assembly of PacBio SMRT reads</h3><p>A total of 28 million PacBio post-filtered reads with an N50 read length of 16 kb was generated from 93 SMRT cells. A read length above 8 kb was used for contig assembly with Falcon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Chin, C. S. et al. Phased diploid genome assembly with single-molecule real-time sequencing. Nat. Methods 13, 1050–1054 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2094">34</a></sup>. The Illumina paired-end reads were mapped to contigs with BWA-MEM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Li, H. Aligning sequence reads, clone sequences and assembly contigs with BWA-MEM. Preprint at arXiv &#xA;https://arxiv.org/abs/1303.3997&#xA;&#xA; (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2098">35</a></sup> and used to polish the assembly with Pilon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Walker, B. J. et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS ONE 9, e112963 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2102">36</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Construction of BioNano optical maps</h3><p>The assembled genome was used to identify restriction sites for BioNano optical maps sequencing with LabelDensityCalculator 1.3.0. BspQ1, a restriction endonuclease that recognizes the target site of GCTCTTC was specifically selected due to its proper label density distributed in the K0326Y genome. The samples were loaded into chips and then the molecules were collected with the BioNano Irys system. The raw BNX files were filtered and assembled into genome maps by using BioNano Solve pipeline (see URLs).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Hybrid assembly of PacBio contigs and BioNano optical maps</h3><p>To make hybrid scaffolds, the previous contigs and the optical maps were aligned using the module ‘HybridScaffold’ in the BioNano Solve pipeline, with the merge P-value of 1 × 10<sup>−11</sup> and alignment length of 80 kb. The gaps were filled by using corrected PacBio reads with PBjelly of the parameters ‘&lt; blasr &gt; -minMatch 8 -minPctIdentity 75 -bestn 1 -nCandidates 20 -maxScore −500 -nproc 4 –noSplitSubreads &lt; /blasr &gt; ’.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Construction of pseudomolecules</h3><p>The reference genome of B73 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Improved maize reference genome with single-molecule technologies. Nature 546, 524–527 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2133">10</a>)</sup> was used to anchor K0326Y scaffolds to chromosomes. Contigs were ordered using NUCmer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Marçais, G. et al. MUMmer4: a fast and versatile genome alignment system. PLoS Comput. Biol. 14, e1005944 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2138">37</a></sup> and placed on chromosomes based on the synteny of K0326Y and B73.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Assembly evaluation</h3><p>The maize GBS-tag sequences (~4.4 million) were mapped against the K0326Y genome with BWA-MEM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Li, H. Aligning sequence reads, clone sequences and assembly contigs with BWA-MEM. Preprint at arXiv &#xA;https://arxiv.org/abs/1303.3997&#xA;&#xA; (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2150">35</a></sup> to evaluate the assembly. The Illumina paired-end data were also used to evaluate the assembly accuracy with the mapping program of SOAP2 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Li, R. et al. SOAP2: an improved ultrafast tool for short read alignment. Bioinformatics 25, 1966–1967 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2154">38</a>)</sup>. Genome completeness was assessed by a number of 1440 single-copy orthologous genes in the Embryophyta dataset of BUSCO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Simao, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210–3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2159">13</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Repetitive element annotation</h3><p>The transposable elements were analyzed with a combination of homolog-based and de novo approaches. The K0326Y-specific TE library was built by RepeatModeler<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Smit, A. F. &amp; Hubley, R. RepeatModeler Open-1.0. &#xA;http://www.repeatmasker.org/&#xA;&#xA; (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2171">39</a></sup>. The final repetitive elements were defined with RepeatMasker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Tarailo‐Graovac, M. &amp; Chen, N. Using RepeatMasker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences. Curr. Protoc. Bioinformatics 25, 4.10.1–4.10.14 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2175">40</a></sup> by using K0326Y-specific and Repbase library<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Jurka, J. et al. Repbase Update, a database of eukaryotic repetitive elements. Cytogenetic Genome Res. 110, 462–467 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2179">41</a></sup>. The full long terminal repeats (LTR) retrotransposons were predicted using LTRharvest<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Ellinghaus, D., Kurtz, S. &amp; Willhoeft, U. LTRharvest, an efficient and flexible software for de novo detection of LTR retrotransposons. BMC Bioinformatics 9, 18 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2183">42</a></sup> and annotated by LTRdigest<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Steinbiss, S., Willhoeft, U., Gremme, G. &amp; Kurtz, S. Fine-grained annotation and classification of de novo predicted LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 37, 7002–7013 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2187">43</a></sup>. <i>Helitrons</i> were predicted by the HelitronScanner program<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Xiong, W., He, L., Lai, J., Dooner, H. K. &amp; Du, C. HelitronScanner uncovers a large overlooked cache of Helitron transposons in many plant genomes. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 111, 10263–10268 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2195">44</a></sup>. SINE-finder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Wenke, T. et al. Targeted identification of short interspersed nuclear element families shows their widespread existence and extreme heterogeneity in plant genomes. Plant Cell 23, 3117–3128 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2199">45</a></sup> was applied to search for tRNA-derived SINEs in the forward strand of the entire genome. Long interspersed nuclear elements (LINEs) were identified using TARGeT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Han, Y., Burnette, J. M. 3rd &amp; Wessler, S. R. TARGeT: a web-based pipeline for retrieving and characterizing gene and transposable element families from genomic sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 37, e78 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2203">46</a></sup>. Terminal inverted repeats (TIRs) were predicted via the pipeline of TARGeT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Han, Y., Burnette, J. M. 3rd &amp; Wessler, S. R. TARGeT: a web-based pipeline for retrieving and characterizing gene and transposable element families from genomic sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 37, e78 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2207">46</a></sup>. MITEs were annotated by MiteHumter<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Han, Y. &amp; Wessler, S. R. MITE-Hunter: a program for discovering miniature inverted-repeat transposable elements from genomic sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 38, e199 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2211">47</a></sup> based on the Matlab toolbox.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Evolutionary dynamics of retrotransposition</h3><p>A unique identifier including the information of superfamily and family was assigned to each structurally defined LTR in the genome. Families were predicted for each TE superfamily using the 80–80–80 rule by implementation in SiLix<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Miele, V., Penel, S. &amp; Duret, L. Ultra-fast sequence clustering from similarity networks with SiLiX. BMC Bioinformatics 12, 116 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2223">48</a></sup> and Vsearch<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Rognes, T., Flouri, T., Nichols, B., Quince, C. &amp; Mahe, F. VSEARCH: a versatile open source tool for metagenomics. PeerJ 4, e2584 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2227">49</a></sup>. The 5’-end sequences of LTR retrotransposons were used to cluster families<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Improved maize reference genome with single-molecule technologies. Nature 546, 524–527 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2231">10</a></sup>. To investigate the evolutionary dynamics of retrotransposition in maize, the consensus sequences from LTR families with 80% divergence were extracted and aligned with MAFFT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Katoh, K. &amp; Standley, D. M. MAFFT multiple sequence alignment software version 7: improvements in performance and usability. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 772–780 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2235">50</a></sup>. The maximum likelihood phylogenetic trees were constructed by RAxML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML version 8: a tool for phylogenetic analysis and post-analysis of large phylogenies. Bioinformatics 30, 1312–1313 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2239">51</a></sup> and visualized via iTOL (<a href="https://itol.embl.de/">https://itol.embl.de/</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Letunic, I. &amp; Bork, P. Interactive tree of life (iTOL) v3: an online tool for the display and annotation of phylogenetic and other trees. Nucleic Acids Res. 44, W242–W245 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2248">52</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Identification of centromeric regions</h3><p>The centromere-related long terminal repeat (CRM), retrotransposons, and the 156-bp tandem repeats (CentC)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Zhong, C. X. et al. Centromeric retroelements and satellites interact with maize kinetochore protein CENH3. Plant Cell 14, 2825–2836 (2002)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2260">14</a></sup> were used to identify the centromeric regions. The CRM sequence was aligned with the K0326Y genomes by BLASTN, with sequence similarity ≥80% and e-value ≤1e–20. After filtering, we used the R script to calculate the candidate centromeric region for chromosomes with 95% confidence interval. The CentC was identified using tandem repeat finder (TRF, Version 4.07b) with the parameters of ‘1 1 2 80 5 200 2000 -d’. More than 10 copies of CentC were defined as the candidate centromere region. Overlapping regions from the two methods were used to determine the centromeric regions.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Iso-seq and RNA-seq</h3><p>To optimize the collection of gene expression data, RNAs from nine tissues (Roots, stems, 12-d seedlings, silks, tassels, ears, whole seeds, callus, and young leaves) were sampled using TRIzol reagent (Invitrogen, Carlsbad, CA) and pooled in equal amounts. Enriched polyA RNA was reversely transcribed into cDNA by using the Clontech SMARTER cDNA synthesis kit, which was subjected to size selection (1~2 kb, 2~3 kb, 3~6 kb, 5~10 kb) using BluePippin. The pooled libraries were sequenced in 40 SMRT cells on the RSII platform and 12 cells on the Sequel platform. PacBio raw data were processed following SMRT analysis using the Iso-seq pipeline to generate Circular Consensus Sequences (CCS). The full-length non-chimeric (FLNC) transcripts were classified and polished using the Iterative Clustering and Error Correction (ICE) algorithm. The high-quality isoforms were mapped to the genome by GMAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Wu, T. D. &amp; Watanabe, C. K. GMAP: a genomic mapping and alignment program for mRNA and EST sequences. Bioinformatics 21, 1859–1875 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2273">53</a></sup>, collapsed with Cupcake ToFU to reduce redundancy. RNA-seq libraries were prepared according the manufacturer’s protocol of Illumina and paired-end sequenced with an insertion size of 350 bp. The reads were assembled using Trinity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Grabherr, M. G. et al. Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-seq data without a reference genome. Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 644 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2277">54</a></sup> and clustered by TIGR<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Pertea, G. et al. TIGR Gene Indices clustering tools (TGICL): a software system for fast clustering of large EST datasets. Bioinformatics 19, 651–652 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2281">55</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Gene annotation</h3><p>The K0326Y genome was annotated with the pipeline of MAKER-P<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Campbell, M. S. et al. MAKER-P: a tool kit for the rapid creation, management, and quality control of plant genome annotations. Plant Physiol. 164, 513–524 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2293">15</a></sup> based on comprehensive evidence from homologous protein sequences, K0326Y transcripts, and ab initio prediction. We used the same repeat masking library that was used for B73 annotation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Improved maize reference genome with single-molecule technologies. Nature 546, 524–527 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2297">10</a></sup>, with the addition of the LTR library annotated by LTRharvest. The gene models were generated by the programs of Augustus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Keller, O., Kollmar, M., Stanke, M. &amp; Waack, S. A novel hybrid gene prediction method employing protein multiple sequence alignments. Bioinformatics 27, 757–763 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2301">56</a></sup> and FGENESH (see URLs) trained for maize and other monocots. The protein sequences were downloaded from Ensemblgenomes release-41, including the species of <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Oryza sativa</i>, <i>Setaria italica</i>, <i>Sorghum bicolor</i>, and <i>Zea mays</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Kersey, P. J. et al. Ensembl Genomes 2018: an integrated omics infrastructure for non-vertebrate species. Nucleic Acids Res. 46, D802–D808 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2320">57</a></sup>. K0326Y transcripts were derived from assembled RNA-seq reads and PacBio isoform sequences. The database of Swiss-Prot, TrEMBL, Interpro, nr, KEGG, KOG, and GO were chosen to assist in assigning functional properties to K0326Y gene models using BLASTP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Camacho, C. et al. BLAST+: architecture and applications. BMC Bioinformatics 10, 421 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2324">58</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">Comparative genomics and structural variation identification</h3><p>Genome alignment among K0326Y, Mo17, and B73 was performed using NUCmer from Mummer package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Kurtz, S. et al. Versatile and open software for comparing large genomes. Genome Biol. 5, R12 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2336">59</a></sup> with the parameters of ‘-mumreference -g 1000 -c 90 -l 40’. The delta-filter was used to remove the mapping noise and determine the one-to-one alignment blocks with parameters ‘-r -q’. SNPs and InDels (&lt;100 bp) were extracted using show-snp in the filtered one-to-one aligned blocks. All these variants were annotated using the ANNOVAR program<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Larkins, B. A., Wu, Y., Song, R. &amp; Messing, J. in Maize Kernel Development (ed. Larkins, B. A.) 175–189 (CABI, Oxfordshire, 2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2340">32</a></sup>. Gene duplications were analyzed with BLASTP (e-value &lt; 1e-10, -v 5, -b 5) for the pairwise similarity measurement and MCscanX package for classification<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Wang, Y. et al. MCScanX: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, e49–e49 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2344">60</a></sup>. The rates of Ka and Ks were calculated with PAML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586–1591 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2348">61</a></sup>. Gene duplications were analyzed with BLASTP for the pairwise similarity measurement and MCscanX package for classification<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Wang, Y. et al. MCScanX: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, e49–e49 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2352">60</a></sup>. The presence/absence variations (PAVs) that was only present in K0326Y but absent in B73/Mo17 were identified as following. We divided the K0326Y genome into 1000-bp overlapping windows with a step size of 200 bp, which was aligned against B73/Mo17 with BWA-MEM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Li, H. Aligning sequence reads, clone sequences and assembly contigs with BWA-MEM. Preprint at arXiv &#xA;https://arxiv.org/abs/1303.3997&#xA;&#xA; (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2357">35</a></sup> and options of ‘-w 1000–M’. The fragments that could not be aligned or alignment coverage was less than 25% were assigned as K0326Y-specific sequences. Then we aligned the CDS of longest transcript to K0326Y-specific sequences by GMAP, and defined CDS regions that covered by K0326Y-specific sequences over 75% as PAV genes. For structural variations, we aligned corrected PacBio long reads from K0326Y against B73 and Mo17 with NGMLR<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Sedlazeck, F. J. et al. Accurate detection of complex structural variations using single-molecule sequencing. Nat. Methods 15, 461–468 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2361">62</a></sup> using default parameters. Structural variations (SVs) were called using Sniffles<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Sedlazeck, F. J. et al. Accurate detection of complex structural variations using single-molecule sequencing. Nat. Methods 15, 461–468 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2365">62</a></sup> when more than 10 reads were supported. Sequences of SVs were retrieved based on their location using BEDTools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Quinlan, A. R. &amp; Hall, I. M. BEDTools: a flexible suite of utilities for comparing genomic features. Bioinformatics 26, 841–842 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2369">63</a></sup>, identified with the ANNOVAR program<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Steinbiss, S., Willhoeft, U., Gremme, G. &amp; Kurtz, S. Fine-grained annotation and classification of de novo predicted LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 37, 7002–7013 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2373">43</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec24">Bulked-segregant analysis</h3><p>K0326Y (QPM) and W64A<i>o2</i> (a classic <i>o2</i> mutant) were used to construct an F<sub>2</sub> population. The extreme vitreous and opaque seeds of F<sub>2</sub> generation were planted in greenhouse. Their DNA samples were screened using the 0707-1 primer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Liu, H. et al. Gene duplication confers enhanced expression of 27-kDa gamma-zein for endosperm modification in quality protein maize. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, 4964–4969 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2395">8</a></sup> and only the ones containing the <i>qr27</i> allele were pooled, amounting to 165 plants with extreme vitreous phenotype and 160 seedlings with extreme opaque phenotype. The DNA library was sequenced with 2 × 150 read lengths and 80-fold depth using the Illumina platform. The raw reads were mapped using BWA-MEM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Li, H. Aligning sequence reads, clone sequences and assembly contigs with BWA-MEM. Preprint at arXiv &#xA;https://arxiv.org/abs/1303.3997&#xA;&#xA; (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2403">35</a></sup> and SNPs were called using GATK<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="McKenna, A. et al. The Genome Analysis Toolkit: a MapReduce framework for analyzing next-generation DNA sequencing data. Genome Res. 20, 1297–1303 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2407">64</a></sup>. A statistical method through calculating the SNP-index and G’ values were applied for detecting the closely linked SNPs for the target traits in each bulk<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Dong, J. et al. Candidate gene identification of existing or induced mutations with pipelines applicable to large genomes. Plant J. 97, 673–682 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2411">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Takagi, H. et al. QTL-seq: rapid mapping of quantitative trait loci in rice by whole genome resequencing of DNA from two bulked populations. Plant J. 74, 174–183 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2414">65</a></sup>. SNP-index and G’ values were calculated in 4 Mb windows with a sliding of 10 kb. To analyze the linkage between the <i>Helitron</i> insertion into the promoter of <i>Pfpα</i> and the vitreous endosperm kernels of the segregating F<sub>2</sub> population, a PCR reaction was performed using a pair of gene specific primers (F: TCTATTTTGCGGTTCTA; R: CTCCCAGCCTAAGCCTC).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec25">Transcriptome differential expression analysis</h3><p>The maize inbred lines of K0326Y, W64A<i>o2</i>, CM105<i>Mo2</i>, and CM105<i>o2</i> were grown in a field in Shanghai, China. Total RNA from endosperm collected at 16 DAP was extracted using TRIzol reagent (Invitrogen). RNA-seq libraries were prepared according to the manufacturer’s protocol for Illumina and sequenced to generate 125-bp paired-end reads using the Illumina HiSeq platform. Adapters and Low-quality reads were removed using Trimmomatic<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Bolger, A. M., Lohse, M. &amp; Usadel, B. Trimmomatic: a flexible trimmer for Illumina sequence data. Bioinformatics 30, 2114–2120 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2444">66</a></sup>. The clean reads were aligned to the maize K0326Y genome using Hisat2 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Kim, D., Langmead, B. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. HISAT: a fast spliced aligner with low memory requirements. Nat. Methods 12, 357 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2448">67</a></sup>) with the built-in Bowtie2 mapping program. Unique mapped reads were counted with htseq-count (HTSeq)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Anders, S., Pyl, P. T. &amp; Huber, W. HTSeq-a Python framework to work with high-throughput sequencing data. Bioinformatics 31, 166–169 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2453">68</a></sup>. The DEseq2 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Anders, S. &amp; Huber, W. Differential expression of RNA-Seq data at the gene level — the DESeq package. &#xA;https://bioconductor.org/packages/release/bioc/vignettes/DESeq/inst/doc/DESeq.pdf&#xA;&#xA; (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d13290e2457">69</a>)</sup> package based on the negative binomial distribution was used to normalize the RNA-seq data and to identify differential expression genes with the standard of the adjusted P-value of 0.05 (Likelihood Ratio Test, sample size <i>n</i> = 3) and the fold-change of more than 1.5×. The PFPα gene expression was also quantified and validated by qRT-PCR reaction by using the specific primers (F: TACTACTTTATTCGGATGATGG; R: CAGAAACCTCCTCGCCTA). The maize <i>Actin</i> gene was used as a reference control (F: GCTACGAGATGCCTGATGGTC, R: CCCCCACTGAGGACAACG).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec26">URLs</h3><p>Arrow, <a href="https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/GenomicConsensus/">https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/GenomicConsensus/</a>; BioNano Solve pipeline, <a href="https://bionanogenomics.com/support-page/bionano-solve/">https://bionanogenomics.com/support-page/bionano-solve/</a>; FastQC, <a href="http://www.bioinformatics.babraham.ac.uk/projects/fastqc/">www.bioinformatics.babraham.ac.uk/projects/fastqc/</a>; FGENESH, <a href="http://www.softberry.com/berry.phtml/">http://www.softberry.com/berry.phtml/</a>; ToFU, <a href="https://github.com/Magdoll/cDNA_Cupcake">https://github.com/Magdoll/cDNA_Cupcake</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec27">Reporting summary</h3><p>Further information on research design is available in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM3">Nature Research Reporting Summary</a> linked to this article.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>Data supporting the findings of this work are available within the paper and its Supplementary Information files. A reporting summary for this Article is available as a Supplementary Information file. The datasets generated and analyzed during the current study are available from the corresponding author upon request. All datasets reported in this study have been deposited in GenBank (NCBI) with the following accession IDs: Genome assembly, VAUS00000000 [<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/CM018593.1">https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/CM018593.1</a>]; Raw data for genome assembly and annotation, <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/PRJNA539996">PRJNA539996</a>; Raw data for Bulked-Segregant Analysis, <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/PRJNA578235">PRJNA578235</a>; and RNA-seq of QPM and o2 maize, <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/PRJNA578012">PRJNA578012</a>. The source data underlying Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM9">2</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM9">4b</a>, as well as Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM9">2</a>b, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM9">5</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM9">9</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM9">10</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM9">13</a>b, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM9">18</a>b, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM9">19</a>b, and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14023-2#MOESM9">21</a> are provided as a Source Data file.</p>

                    Google Scholar</a> 
                </p></li><li class="c-article-references__item js-c-reading-companion-references-item" itemprop="citation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/ScholarlyArticle"><meta itemprop="author" content="Y.
